0199562822|5.0|[[VIDEOID:mo1BGR0R8V5OS4Y]]<br /><br />ON FORENSIC PSYCHIATRY FOR LEGAL AND MEDICAL PROFESSIONALS<br /><br />An appreciation by Phillip Taylor MBE and Elizabeth Taylor of Richmond Green Chambers<br /><br />Distinguished by its thoroughness and clarity, this superb handbook guides practitioners through the sometimes miasmic overlap between psychiatry and law.  It's particularly and obviously relevant to legal practitioners who either prosecute or represent mentally disordered patients, or those who may be suspected of having a mental disorder.<br /><br />Forensic psychiatry, as the term indicates, is cross-disciplinary.  It's defined by the learned authors as comprising `the psychiatry of mental disorder and offending behaviours, plus law in relation to all psychiatry'.  Both of these aspects are represented in this handbook.<br /><br />`It is not possible either effectively or ethically,' say the authors,  `to guide an offender patient through the justice system... or properly to assist the justice system in dealing with mentally disordered offenders, without a real understanding of legal process and of the interface between law and psychiatry'.<br /><br />The stated aim of the authors is to summarise and describe areas of law which are relevant to this interface, thus rendering the handbook of immense value, not just to lawyers, but for clinicians whose work is regulated by the law.<br /><br />Part of the Oxford Medical Publications Library published by the Oxford University Press, this handbook of almost 700 pages, is actually the first edition of `Forensic Psychiatry' and like the other specialist handbooks in psychiatry published by the OUP, it's formatted as a quick reference tool -- compact yet authoritative and complete - and designed to save the busy practitioner valuable time.<br /><br />It's written by a distinguished team of forensic psychiatrists and psychologists working in consultation with over thirty other experts who have contributed their advice, including, certainly, psychiatrists and psychologists, as well as lawyers, judges and academics.<br /><br />Here -- to cite just one example -- is where, if you're a lawyer, you would look for guidance in understanding the psychiatrist's court report, or the risk assessment offered to the court.<br /><br />The Handbook is divided into five parts covering, for example in Parts II and III, clinical forensic psychiatry and the ethics of forensic psychiatry.  Parts IV and V examines the law relevant to psychiatry, and then psychiatry within the legal system.  There are no less than six appendices providing convenient reference to diagnostic classifications, statutes, legal cases, ethical codes and important inquiries and reports.<br /><br />There's also a quick reference to mental health law and a detailed index at the back for further ease of use.  For clear, concise guidance on all aspects of forensic psychiatry, this is the ideal handbook for a wide range of professionals dealing with mental health in both the legal and medical professions, and it's a fundamental tool for advocates in these hard cases.ncluding, certainly, psychiatrists and psychologists, as well as lawyers, judges and academics.<br /><br />Here -- to cite just one example -- is where, if you're a lawyer, you would look for guidance in understanding the psychiatrist's court report, or the risk assessment offered to the court.<br /><br />The Handbook is divided into five parts covering, for example in Parts II and III, clinical forensic psychiatry and the ethics of forensic psychiatry. B005FFW46S|4.0|I will say up front that this is, as with all the Discorld books, absolutely an enjoyable book, a real pleasure to read. Nonetheless, in a lot of ways I definitely felt that this was one of the weakest of the Discworld novels and of the Sam Vimes books in particular. The primary problem I feel, as some others have pointed out, is that the focus of the book is entirely on Sam Vimes himself, a character who began and has always excelled as a part of an ensemble and an underdog. At this point in the series however, Vimes has been through so much and has become such a prominent figure on the Disc, that he has almost lost everything that made him interesting. He no longer has to struggle to succeed, but merely does so by simply being better than everyone else, especially with the added supernatural element of having a demonic force trapped inside that frequently aids him. In a sense, he's become the Discworld's Batman; the grim, driven, unstoppable crime-fighter that always comes out on top, leaving all sense of actual danger and suspense behind because, at this point, Sam Vimes could breathe in space.<br />So Vimes is dragged to the Ramkin estate in the country for a holiday, against his protests. Of course we expect him to uncover some kind of crime when he gets there, but rather than actually uncovering a crime he basically just announces as soon as he gets there that crime is afoot. How does he know this? Because he's Sam Vimes and he's just that clever.<br />Throughout the book we see him casually dispatching every single antagonist he encounters. He goes into town and is challenged to a fight with a man twice his size and half his age, and he quickly and efficiently defeats him. Why? Because he's Sam Vimes and he's just better. Of course a real villain is eventually discovered, a vicious murderer with absolutely no regard for life, and of course Vimes is able to best him at every encounter, because he's Sam Vimes and he's just better.<br />To his credit though, Pratchett is very much aware of this, as much of the book is a reflection on just how far Vimes has risen over the course of the series. He's unstoppable because he's already been through everything else, which in a way makes this book's greatest weakness also its greatest strength. Vimes is here portrayed as an entirely infallible protagonist, but he's also earned his right to be that infallible protagonist. Vimes has come such a long way that there really is nowhere else for him to go, except on holiday. And in that sense I feel that, if there are never anymore books about Sam Vimes, than this makes for a fitting end to his career.

0345803485|4.0|\\"Fifty Shades of Grey,\\" the first in a trilogy of supposed-erotica seems more a psychological study of one woman's choice regarding what she truly desires from a relationship with a man than a tale of twisted sex.<br /><br />First person narrator, Anastasia Steele, the young, newly-graduated college girl with a degree in English literature, knows what kind of man turns her on--Angel Clare in Hardy's \\"[[ASIN:014043514X Tess of the D'Urbervilles (Penguin Classics)]]\\" or maybe Mr. Darcy of \\"[[ASIN:0486284735 Pride and Prejudice (Dover Thrift Editions)]].\\"  Nevertheless, despite her romantic inclinations, Ana is unprepared for the electricity generated between her and Christian Grey, a young and extremely wealthy entrepreneur she interviews while substituting for her journalist roommate. E.L. James' Grey smolders with hot intensity--he's movie star attractive with his own helicopter, jet and multi-billion dollar company--what's not to like?  Poor Ana does not have a chance, he has her way before `hello' or the multitude of text messages and emails the two compulsively share.  Grey's only black mark is his penchant for rough sex.  Like, I said, poor Ana--helicopters, jets, a gifted Mac, a Blackberry,  a car and a smarting rear end.  Ouch!<br /><br />Like all young women, Ana seems surprised at the power she seems to hold over Grey's stylishly tousled head.  She cannot get over how fetchingly Grey's pants hang off his slender hips.  James' frequent interjection of an italicized \\"Oh my\\" whenever Ana is sexually or emotionally titillated brings the thrill of first love or lust as experienced by a girl of Ana's age brilliantly alive.  But like most women, Ana is guilty of the most typical relationship mistake.  She assumes that through her love and devotion--her acquiescence to all of Grey's desires--she will eventually convince Grey of the vastness of her infinite and selfless love and that he, in turn, will share every aspect of himself with her as he has done with no other. She thinks she can change him.  Ha!  Danger, Will Robinson!<br /><br />Do women ever really think of the kind of life they will have with a man that they think they constantly have to \\"work on?\\"  Imagine thirty years of asking Grey, \\"can I touch you today?\\"  or \\"is today the day you love me enough to share your psychosis?\\"  No thanks!  In this sense, James' narrative is quite realistic about Ana's naivety; her portrayal of Ana as a young woman wanting so much to please at the expense of her own thought regarding love and what works for her rings true. Ana deludes herself until she realizes that simple is much better.  No fighting and no biting also means no doubt.<br /><br />With that all in mind, and with the knowledge that eventually Ana realizes that although she is drawn to the handsome albeit damaged Grey, she cannot accept his less-than-perfect terms and so moves out of his scene with her integrity intact, why does this simple story generate so much negativity?  It can't be because the book thrums with such scandalously kinky sex!  In this age of Internet porn and relaxed moralities, this tale of slightly forbidden fruit is a bit freeze-dried. Even teenage-targeted television series that advocate multiple partners and so many other emotionally challenging issues that would put even the most savvy and world-weary sophisticate into a quagmire of hysteria erode their audiences in a much more damaging way--through repeated exposure that breeds a redefinition of what is simply bad behavior.  Keeping things simple and true to self insures happiness not only on a Dali Lama spiritual level but on a grounded awareness platform of what works and what does not.<br /><br />The fact that the origin of \\"Fifty Shades\\"  is \\"Twilight\\" fan fiction at one time entitled \\"Master of the Universe,\\" should not come as any surprise; Ana's view of Grey rivals that of Bella's regard of Edward.  Both Grey and Edward fit into the \\"monster\\" category--one sucks blood, the other wants todraw blood.  Both women will prove to be the sole salvation for these enigmatic men.  Both foster an increased self-confidence in the two female narrators.  The premise of both these novels seem to empower woman who wish to be the thorn in the side of controlling men and ultimately turn these men into devoted docile partners.<br /><br />Disregarding the connection between Twilight and 50 Shades, and regarding the novel as just an escapist read, could it be that James is capitalizing on this need of certain women to fantasize--overtake a man at his own game--and just simply writing an entertaining story?  Of course, Grey is an obviously damaged human spirit who will (in books two and three of the trilogy) eventually heal through the burning light of Ana's steadfast and trustful love.  That makes the outcome more reasonable.  Grey is wounded.  Ana nurtures; Ana heals.  If the novel is analyzed with this in mind, then perhaps it is not the quintessential piece of erotica that it is touted to be.  Sure, \\"50 Shades\\" contains many sexual interludes--some of which yawn with a ho-hum read-this-kind-of-stuff-before-and-skipped-over-it quality (heck--Diana Gabaldon's Outlander series has plenty of sexual liaisons, but no one is squawking about those thousand page tomes).  Giving Ms. James the benefit of the doubt, perhaps the actual theme of the novel is about making the right choice for oneself.  If Ana was a different sort of gal, right or wrong, maybe she would have picked another outcome for her shared life with Grey.  This Ana doesn't want to be submissive--she is true to herself and leaves.  How many other women or men would have just hunkered into a luxurious lifestyle?  Why let a cat-o-nine tail stop you from material success?<br /><br />Why is the novel being touted as [[ASIN:B003VPX1XY Lady Chatterley's Lover (The Unexpurgated Edition)]] for 2012?  Just to sell books!  Don't expect \\"[[ASIN:0802131786 Tropic of Cancer]]\\" or the famed \\"[[ASIN:0156260255 The Diary of Anais Nin, Vol. 1: 1931-1934]]\\" by the other \\"Ana,\\" Anais Nin.  \\"50 Shades\\" isn't about exploration--it's about trying out something alien in an attempt to understand another person--a psychologically damaged person.  And sadly, there are plenty of real people out there who are \\"fifty shades of Tom,\\" \\"fifty shades of Bill\\" or \\"fifty shades of Joey\\"--men who have been scalded emotionally and view the sexual act as a means of ultimate control where pleasure is expressed through pain.  Alas and sigh.  Way too complicated for me.<br /><br />Bottom line?  Is \\"Fifty Shades of Grey\\" an awful sophomoric attempt at erotica?  It is, what it is--a novel relating the experience of a young girl who falls in love with a man who has been emotionally hurt and can only release himself through the deliverance of pain. For sure, the novel contains sex that takes place in a room designed with the Marquis de Sade in mind.  However, James' Grey seems to be on the brink of understanding a different kind of love through the selfless ministrations of his Ana. Will she go back to him?  Will he be healed?  The answer to this is as simple as Twilight's \\"Who will Bella choose?\\" conundrum.  The key word here is \\"entertainment.\\"  Warning:  \\"Fifty Shades of Grey\\" contains many sexually explicit scenes complete with crude language and hints at some sexual habits considered by some to be on the far side of wildness. Recommended for those who view the book as entertainment--not Grey Matter!  View it as the story of a young girl who must make a serious choice without losing her integrity.<br />Added Comments: After reading about two thirds of the second book of the Grey trilogy, I realize that, sadly, Ana isn't very wise.  She's just another kid who thinks she can be \\"the one\\" to change another guy with baggage using the tagline \\"but, I love him.\\"  Grey seems to transform under her \\"loving\\" hand which, I guess, is author James' ultimate fantasy.  The draw, I suppose, is to figure out why Grey is the way he is.  Unfortunately as he becomes more \\"feeling,\\" the book stutters in its routine: Ana teaches; Grey learns; Sex; Ana persists; Grey reveals; Sex . . . a contiguous loop that enchants less.<br />Diana Faillace Von Behren<br />\\"reneofc\\"nais Nin, Vol. 1: 1931-1934]]\\" by the other \\"Ana,\\" Anais Nin.  \\"50 Shades\\" isn't about exploration--it's about trying out something alien in an attempt to understand another person--a psychologically damaged person. 0316010669|3.0|Malcolm Gladwell is an essayist who made his bones working for the New Yorker.  His first book, THE TIPPING POINT, proved to be a pretty nice commercial and critical success.  BLINK, the follow up, seems to stray away from the quality of his debut.  BLINK: The Power of Thinking Without Thinking is a pop-psychology book whose main argument is that human judgment is best when its immediate.  Gladwell's assertion is that our immediate reaction to events are often the most appropriate. The opposite claim is made as well -- we seem to make our worst judgments when we dwell on them and think about them critically.<br /><br />Gladwell always has an engaging and captivating voice.  In his essays, he has the ability to take some of the most seemingly mundane subjects and make them a fascinating topic to think about.  BLINK is no buck from this trend; it is very well written, and there's a lot here to think about.  The biggest problem with this book is the biggest problem with many of his essays, and in particular, his third book OUTLIERS; he works to prove his hypotheses rather than disprove other possible alternatives.  Gladwell uses an assortment of experiments to \\"prove\\" that our snap judgments are more valuable than otherwise, but he never really gives much credence to the myriad of evidence that suggests otherwise.  He gives a few examples, a few anecdotes, a few interesting stories, and that's all the evidence the reader should be expected to want, right?<br /><br />Unfortunately, BLINK proved to not be very convincing.  It did, however, prove to be a very fun and engaging book.  Even though I was not quite convinced, the ideas presented here are fun to think about.  I'd recommend BLINK to other fans of Gladwell's work (or popular science in general), but as always, take it with a grain of salt.

0307352145|4.0|The general consensus is that if a person is quiet, or not very talkative in a group setting, then they are not the ones in control, or don't have the best ideas.  However, Susan Cain goes to great lengths to discuss introversion as a cultural, psychological, sociological, and personal phenomenon.  I love looking at things that way.  She shows how it is the particular insights of people who are quiet enough to study and articulate problems and circumstances that have the power to change things and make big things happen.  Unfortunately, our culture is designed to encourage and reward extroverts and their contributions, which may not necessarily be the most well thought out or beneficial for society.  The ramifications for this are also explored in detail.<br /><br />One of the most interesting threads for me was the discussion of how churches are set up and geared almost exclusively toward extroverts.  One example--the 'meet and greet' time that is part of the worship service of churches all over.  I HATE this.  Absolutely hate it.  And it has nothing to do with being an introvert.  I just know that it makes all kinds of people uncomfortable, and gives them yet another reason to check out of church.  I mean, seriously, how does that make people feel comfortable and welcome?  If people want to talk to or 'visit with their neighbor', then they will make a point of doing on their own, not because someone in the front instructed everybody to do it at the specified time.<br /><br />Quiet was an interesting book, and one that I think is of particular importance for people in organizational leadership.  Diverse voices may be quiet, but those voices are important nonetheless.<br /><br />I received a copy of this book from WaterBrook Multnomah for review purposes.  No other compensation was received.

It shows the ugly of Nazi Germany, the Nazis and Hitler, as well as the beautiful of Nazi Germany, the people that do not support Hitler and the innocent victims of the Holocaust.<br /><br />  It is narrated by Death.  Death tells the story from an omniscient point of view.  It is a perfect book for Death to narrate, considering it takes place during WWII in Nazi Germany.  Death remembers certain events with colors, for example during a battle scene, Death remembers red.  He is humorous at some points and is also sarcastic.  This lightens the mood, considering the book can be depressing and dark at times.  Death sees the beautiful and ugly in people, but prefers to find the beautiful.  He says that humans misunderstand him.  Despite what you might think, Death does not like war and really just needs a vacation.  He says he is more human than people think.<br /><br />  Even though you only hear from Death, the main character is Liesel Meminger, the book thief.  She moves in with a foster family when she is nine after her younger brother dies.  Her foster mom is Rosa Hubermann, a foul mouthed but loving mother.  Rosa is strict, but at the same time she loves Liesel and just wants the best for her.  Her foster father is Hans Hubermann.  He is an accordion player, who is addicted to cigarettes.  Hans is against the Nazi party and tries to avoid joinging it.  Liesel likes Hans better and finds him easier to get to know than Rosa.<br /><br />  Throughout the novel, Liesel acquires books by either stealing them or getting them as gifts.  Hans teaches Liesel how to read a little with the first book that she steals.  Her love of books causes her to steal more, and she meets and befriends people while doing so.  One of those friends is Rudy.  Rudy is Liesels age, and is her neighbor.  Rudy falls in love with Liesel and is always trying to get Liesel to love him back.  Another friend that Liesel has is Max.  Max is Jewish and hides with the Hubermanns during the Holocaust.  He writes Liesel books and they together realize how powerful words are.<br /><br />  I enjoyed the Book Thief.  It can be sad, but it really makes you think.  It is interesting how much a young girl from a small German town sees in just a couple of years. He tells the story of Liesel as he is taking the souls of dead people around her. Because Death is the narrator, the mood of the story is obviously very sad and solemn. The main characters of this novel are Liesel Meminger (the book thief), Hans and Rosa Hubermann, Max Vandenburg, and Rudy Steiner. Liesel is the foster daughter of Hans and Rosa and goes to live with them after her brother dies and her mother gives her away to the Hubermanns. Max is a Jewish fist fighter that is hiding from the Nazis in the Hubermann's basement and he soon becomes friends with Liesel. The intended audience of this novel is everyone. Everybody can easily read and enjoy this novel without any problems because it is so gripping and easy to keep up with. I loved this book because of the historical background and the story within it. This is the first story that I have read that was from this distinct point of view and that contained such a strong message all throughout the novel. I agree that words can be a truly powerful thing to use. Liesel used words throughout the book because she read many books and that is one of the things that helped her pull through such a rough time. Words can be especially powerful and this story is perfect proof of it. I specifically liked how the author compared Liesel and Hitler with the way that they use their words because Hitler was such a masterful speaker and that is the reason that he was able to have so many followers. By using this comparison, Zusak is saying that Liesel is just as powerful. The author did a magnificent job of writing this novel because he wrote through every aspect of literature. This book could not be improved in any way because it is such a masterpiece already.

How many \\"rock\\" bands today occupy that rare space between pop-radio friendly and hard core metal? As a band that has, at one time or another, walked many hallways and could be stuck with many labels, once again these guys refuse to be pigeon-holed. The Cult returns with an album that is relevant, invigorating and inspiring. From the energy of \\"The Wolf\\" and \\"For the Animals\\" to the sonic \\"Elemental Light\\" and funky \\"A Pale Horse\\", The Cult move all around the place and just play music the way they want to play it. I hear all sorts of things in here; some old school Iggy Pop, some vibes from the \\"Love\\" era, the swagger of \\"Beyond Good & Evil\\" and even some \\"Electric\\" tones. Lyrically there seem to be lots of elements about forgiveness, survival, hope.<br /><br />Ian is in top form as vocalist, growling and spitting out his lyrics. He really seems to be at a cool place mentally, much more than I think I've ever seen him. Billy maintains his place as the strong-armed guitarist, cranking out solid grooves with bravado and intensity. Duffy always draws me in with his guitar playing and he's in top form here. Tempesta is a great drummer and really fits well with his percussion. Mr. Wyse fills the bass slot perfectly.<br /><br />I've been a long time fan and I'm amazed that they are back with so much energy and organization. Musicians like this remind me that you don't have to get apathetic while growing old. The search for meaning and new things is a life long process that we should all work tirelessly for. Billy was quoted as saying that this album was really for their fans and it shows. Thanks guys for delivering a great piece of work. So proud of you.

1401235417|5.0|Lots of writers have over the decades taken lots of different approaches to Batman.  Impressive enough as a fine prose writer, Snyder's \\"The Court of Owls\\" will certainly earn him a place among the highest tier of authors to riff on the Dark Knight, indeed, it may well signal a place to come in that pantheon of truly extraordinary comic book maestros.<br /><br />In \\"Court of Owls,\\" Snyder is firing on every cylinder.  He creatively combines two recurring Batman plot elements for which I have a particular weakness (Gotham City as more than a setting but as a character and Bruce Wayne's first love, along with further exploring the history of the Wayne family).  Just as Bruce Wayne launches a major urban renewal program, his evening half is confronted by a mysterious murder.  The murder evokes \\"The Court of Owls,\\" a Gotham legend captured in a nursery rhyme.  According to the myth, the Court of Owls is a secret society that \\"watches\\" over Gotham.  Batman has investigated the whether the Court exists and has determined it to be a myth -- but was he right?<br /><br />One cannot say much more without risking spoilers (several reviews here get too close to that line for my taste), but suffice to say, Snyder works wonders.  On top of a very solid plot and a gripping story arc, his sense of Bruce Wayne as a character is extraordinary -- a man cut off from his emotions, able to pretend during the day and unleash his rage by night -- as well as what promises to be a vital and complex perspective on Dick Grayson.  He also manages to incorporate some of the more metaphysical elements of the Batman mythos (owls prey on bats), without it feeling anything less than organic to the story.  Snyder also demonstrates an awareness of the fact that, more perhaps than with other comic heroes, suspense is a vital element in a successful batman story.<br /><br />On a last note, I'd be negligent if I failed to mention artist Greg Capullo.  His style, toned down some from his Spawn days, suitsSnyder's vibe like a bat in a cave.  It is dark and evocative and at times almost verging on the florid, yet maintains a sense of grim reality that leaves a reader worried about the consequences of every choice and turn.  One thing is certain, whatever your worries about DCs new reboot, the Caped Crusader has landed in the hands of a pair of spectacular talents!  No true Batman fan will want to miss this book.

In fact, he further breaks it down into one sentence: \\"The art of economics consists in looking not merely at the immediate but at the longer effects of any act or policy; it consists in tracing the consequences of that policy not merely for one group but for all groups.\\"<br /><br />The \\"lesson\\" portion of the book is only four pages long, but is followed up by nearly 200 pages of application of this lesson to a number of circumstances. For those who think that economics is boring, I hope you'll take me at my word: this book is not boring! Not only does Hazlitt succeed in speaking clearly for the layman; he also makes a sometimes difficult subject truly fascinating. B0036D7Y20|5.0|Kempe did not record enough but this recording alone would be enough to preserve his reputation for posterity. He had a gift for maintaining an over-arching shape throughout an opera without allowing the big moments to become desiccated or smoothed over; hence he move effortlessly from the serene sheen of the VPO strings in the Prelude to the muttering menace of the Act II Prelude and preserve dramatic unity.  The VPO still had a highly individual, distinctive sound in the early 60's, not least in the rasp of their woodwind and the dark roundness of their lower strings and they clearly relish playing under Kempe, who had by this time transferred his loyalties to them from Berlin.<br /><br />EMI's casting was as good as could be made in the early 60's; especially striking is Christa Ludwig's Ortrud. Many great artists - Astrid Varnay, Margarete Klose, Gwyneth Jones - have made a triumph of this role but Ludwig, just at that point in her career when she had a brilliant top and extraordinary dark heft in the lower reaches of her mezzo-soprano. Furthermore, her chemistry with the just recently deceased Dietrich Fischer-Dieskau's Friedrich is palpable: he craven, blustering and insecure, she steely and imperturbably malignant. I am a non-fan of D-F-D but concede that this is among his finest and most apt characterisations. In fact there is only one serious weakness in the cast: Otto Wiener's elderly-sounding, nasal Herald, so dry and unprepossessing of voice compared with, say, the stentorian Tom Krause for Sawallisch at Bayreuth. Elisabeth Gr&uuml;mmer is ideal: silvery, vulnerable, ethereal - perfect. Jess Thomas allows the occasional intrusive bleat to mar his line but is otherwise heroic and impassioned. Gottlob Frick is suitably imposing as Henry the Fowler.<br /><br />There are other very recommendable recordings, live, under von Matacic, Sawallisch and Kubelik's studio version but this one holds its ranking as pre-eminent in that it achieves a grandeur and spiritual dimension beyond any other.al dimension beyond any other.
0375842209|5.0|The brilliantly written novel The Book Thief by Markus Zusak is about a family hiding a Jew while living in Nazi Germany during World War II. The Book Thief is a historical fiction book, using factual information from World War II, but adding in the story of a fictional family. Throughout the novel the tone is very serious and mildly depressing when events such as death occur. Oddly enough, the whole novel is narrated by Death himself, which adds a point of view different than most readers are used to. The main characters in The Book Thief are Liesel Meminger, Rudy Steiner, and Max Vandenburg. Liesel and Rudy are best friends who grow up together in the same neighborhood and have about the same family situations. Liesel's family meets Max when he is forced to leave his family and hide in the Hubermanns basement in order to escape from the Nazis. The Book Thief is a novel intended for age groups in high school and above. Some of the parts in this novel are too complex and depressing for younger age groups to understand. This book is so intriguing because it is very easy to connect with all the characters on a personal level. I specifically like how Death is the narrator because I have never read another book where I got to see life through the eyes of Death. The theme of The Book Thief is the strength that unlikely compassion has between some characters in the novel. The purpose in writing this book was to give a personal example of a family hiding a Jewish man in Nazi Germany during World War II. Markus Zusak does an incredible job getting his point across throughout the novel. Markus gives specific details for each character in a way that makes you either like or dislike them depending on whether or not Markus wants you to. I would honestly give this book five stars as it is because it is so interesting and emotional that it left me wanting more. The Book Thief is one of the most interesting books I have ever read, and I would definitely recommend it to any readerlooking for an exciting emotional rollercoaster!

1400032717|5.0|The Curious Incident of the Dog in the Night-time has quickly become an addition to my list of favorite books. Mark Haddon manages to blend humor, sadness, and suspense in an intricate puzzle of a novel that leaves you amazed. At the beginning of the book, you might believe a small child is telling the story due to the simplistic language and innocent observations, but eventually you realize the narrator, Christopher, is a 15 year old boy. It is never said explicitly in the novel, but Christopher exhibits the mental impairments of someone with an autistic spectrum disorder. He<br />is literal to a fault, highly logical, socially impaired, and cannot stand physical contact.  This novel really forces you to think about what life is like for someone like this who is trapped inside his own mind. Christopher is a genius in so many regards; he is highly observant, can do complex math equations faster than most of us can do simple arithmetic, and exhibits a Sherlock-Holmes-esque ability to work through the logistics of his world. Christopher is brilliant in all these regards, but he misses out on many creative and social experiences in his life due to his mental impairment. The reader can see his thought processes, listen to the way he thinks, and understand how logical and sound his mind is as it works through his life. However, we also see how odd his behavior actually is in relation to the real world, no matter how rational his thoughts are.  I found this unique viewpoint and writing style incredibly compelling. This book is a mystery in more ways than one, and with such a limited, fragmented view of the world, as shown through the lens of Christopher's unique perspective, the puzzle is even more rewarding when the reader can eventually piece it together. The Curious Incident of the Dog in the Night-time is a fascinating read that I would recommend to anyone who enjoys a classic mystery employing a deductive reasoning style and a new kind of narrative perspective.

As in, when you try zooming in to the full size of your screen (iPad or android tablets, or cloud reader) it's still really small, and it's difficult to judge good lighting/focus/sharpness with a low resolution image. Which is very important in a photography book, especially when he gives so many examples. To compare, take a look at the sample for [[ASIN:1118138228 Night and Low-Light Photography Photo Workshop]]. The photos in the book allow you to zoom in and see the details he can capture with the lighting, which I repeat, is very important for photography books, especially when your book is all about lighting and exposure!<br /><br />I'm not gonna cover too much in terms of what other people have said - there will be agreements and disagreements on people's opinions on how the author presents the information in his style and what not. Here's my very brief take:<br /><br />He does a good job of urging people to use manual focus. However, initially he comes off as \\"you don't need to use Av or Tv mode, just use manual\\" but if you read through it, you'll notice that he does use Av mode quite often in his photos. His justification is when the lighting is really good, your camera is smart enough to get the correct exposure with Av/Tv mode. If you want my advice, if you're not that fast or experienced with manual, then use it when you have the time to get practice, but if you're in a setting where you're constantly taking photos, such as at a birthday party or of your pets, just use what is most comfortable to you (which will probably be Av or automatic). Having photos for memories of these events is more important and you can practice elsewhere until you have the experience and skill to take full manual photos.<br /><br />I disagree strongly with his preference to slightly underexpose photos rather than overexpose (when you can't get the \\"perfect\\" exposure of course). Perhaps this is because he comes from the film age, but with digital, your digital sensor is much more sensitive and captures much more information on the \\"light\\" side, so slightly overexposing will give you much more detail and allows you to lower the exposure/lighting in post-production (e.g., by using \\"recovery\\" in lightroom).  If you try to add light in post production, you end up with a lot of noise because your sensor didn't capture the detail.

B004DK5CW4|2.0|[A polite reminder: disagreeing with the gist of reviews is no grounds to deem them 'unhelpful.' Vote against only if their perspective is poorly supported or confusingly articulated. In dialogue with another Amazon reviewer I was shocked to learn that she deleted reviews that lowered her scores, as they impaired her status as a \\"Top\\" reviewer. In fact, she no longer writes negative reviews to avoid this! There's something wrong with that, isn't there? Please consider this before you vote. Use the Comments to air any disagreements.]<br /><br />`Secretariat' isn't among the best in the long line of movies about racetracks and horses and the people who run them. That's saying a lot, since it's got the best story of them all, at least given its protagonist, the near-miraculous Secretariat, and it's clearly among the best-funded film ever about horses and racetracks. It also has the benefit of some very solid casting, including Diane Lane, a fine actress who's a dream to watch, and John Malkovich, from the very top drawer. Despite its Disney roots, a weak script and indifferent direction hamper this movie with one-dimensional characters and low production standards.<br /><br />The weakest link is some very feeble characterizations. In one of its best scenes, the Lane character's main adversary is too easily bested by her in a public forum where their repartee comes closest to sparkling. That rival is otherwise confined to growling and shaking his fist at her from a distance, and yet he is the nearest thing in this film to a colorful secondary character of interest. No-one thought to include more of them, to lend some panache to this movie: no cigar-chomping Roscoes worrying over a racing form; no sweaty gamblers being henpecked in the stands by blowsy broads; no allowance-risking teens playing hooky for a taste of the track. Nada. A smart-alecky jockey might have added spice, as would giving the stable-boy more than just two or three lines - at least two of which he addresses to the horse!<br /><br />With such a top-flight cast even this might have been okay had the leads been put to better use. But the script is limp almost throughout, characterizing the protagonists through trite observations. The Malkovich character's inept golfing and garish outfits are subject to some ribbing - although nobody flinches when at least once he inexplicably appears in something quite stylish. Also, the dialogue between Lane and her hippie daughter too often involves heartfelt compliments about each other's good looks. Yawn.<br /><br />We're told that this daughter's youthful activism mirrors her mother's strength of spirit, but this goes no farther, hardly helping to develop either character. In another lurking subplot, or so it might seem, the protagonist lives away from her husband but with a rather masculine woman who happens to elicit some of her warmest smiles, and at least once even embraces her consolingly. But if you begin to reckon that something will emerge from this, think again: it, too, adds up to nothing (...maybe in the book?).<br /><br />Again, not much is striking in the art direction. Costumes and settings for this presumably early 1970s story are okay but don't stand out. The DVD's added features focus on how the racing was filmed from up close, deftly from a hanging camera, from the jockey's viewpoint, etc. Crafty, but nothing in this film quite conveys the exoticism of racetracks, which are elegant places where fortunes are hopefully made and lives are often broken - and this story is, precisely, about a big gamble. Interest could have been ratcheted up with a hand-held sweep through some echoing grand hall, especially of action at a racetrack's betting wickets, to show these near-mythical venues' excitement and atmosphere. But this last is what this production most lacks. In fact, like Lane's oddly frequent telephoned interactions with both husband and daughter, most of the racetrack scenes were probably filmed in a studio.Sadly, it shows.<br /><br />This may be one for the kids; just be sure they're very young. 0765316994|4.0|John Scalzi never disappoints. He writes very solid and entertaining stories, but with Redshirts he's letting his geek flag fly at all-time heights. Even further than he did with The Android's Dream. If you've been a fan of Sci-Fi and Star Trek: TOS in particular then you understand the idea of being a red shirt means you're probably going to die or at least be relegated to a bit player. Redshirts shows us a future where being issued a wardrobe of red is a very bad thing.<br /><br />It is true that Redshirts skewers early Trek and it does it very, very well. And it contains the best BJ joke you've probably heard all year. Yet Scalzi goes much deeper as things progress and just gets out and out strange in the last third. The story begins simply enough with new ensign level crew members coming aboard the Universal Union flagship Intrepid, which just always seems to be running low on crew. They quickly realize they are the expendable type, but something more seems to be going on then that. Improbable things keep happening and then things get meta and weird. Or is all meta weird?<br /><br />The story is not without its faults. Very little character growth occurs and some of the characters aren't very indistinguishable from others. There are many instances of some clunky bits especially in the aforementioned final third. However, this hardly ever dampened the grin the story continual caused me to sport. Either way Scalzi has written the book he's probably been dying to get to for years and the Star Trek parody I've always wanted. And while it is in a similar camp as Galaxy Quest it's setup rather differently and explores the trappings of Sci-Fi in a very unusual manner. To go into it much more would ruin things. Suffice to say if the initial hook works for you I can't help but recommend this to most Sci-Fi fans, especially those of an older generation that are looking for more than a few laughs.<br /><br />It is a much stronger showing then Fuzzy Nation, but still doesn't reach the level of detail or characterization that the Old Man's War books achieve. Redshirts is most definitely Scalzi's lightest and funniest novel to date. I give Redshirts 4 out of 5 hats. The story ends with 3 codas that are ancillary short stories closing off some loose ends brought up in the main narrative. The first was overly long and the third felt unneeded, but these parts are the more emotionally fueled sections.

0374203032|5.0|Here is nice little book by Michael Sandel, who I first heard about on the Colbert Report. His main question is what he ends the book with, \\"Do we want a society where everything is up for sale? Or are there certain moral and civic goods that markets do not honor and money cannot buy?\\" He is speaking about our society and what type of one do we want to live in. It is a book which begs the question of how do markets reflect our morality and in what ways do they shape it. I really enjoyed the way Sandel writes this book with many case studies and arguments on both sides. While you can definitely see they way in which he thinks toward each situation, it seems he presents it more with the notion that these issues are something we need to realize and think critically about before they in turn shape who we become. Mainly using his two fold rubric of fairness and corruption, he assesses different situations to determine their moral judgement and how that relates to a market judgement. I think this raises some great questions to utilitarianism and different ways we make decisions. He is tying morality into everything we do, especially in the free market world, where not even here are you free from moral decisions. Rather we need to have these moral and civic discussions. Markets should not take a \\"nonjudgmental stance toward values.\\" This is my overall impression and short summary, below I will give a chapter by chapter breakdown if you are interested.<br /><br />Jumping the Queue<br /><br />Here Sandel brings up the line cutting issue of whether there are certain things we should all have to wait for, or can money buy a fast track. This includes jumping lines at airports, solo carpool drivers, ticket scalping, concierge doctors, and the new line standing business. Are there certain things that all should wait their turn for, or does the childhood rule \\"don't cut in line\\" not apply to us so grown up? He also speaks to some of the other ways we allocate things as merit, need, and others.<br /><br />Incentives<br /><br />Incentives are the name of the game of economics. I am not really a student of economics though I do know my way around moral and ethical issues, and this was perhaps my favorite chapter. He raises some tough questions and starts the chapter off with one of the toughest, asking whether drug addicted women should be offered incentives to become sterile. I still don't really know where I stand but here was brought up some great discussion. Being in education I agree with his assessment of corruption coming with paying students for reading or getting good grades, but I also see parallels in this and certain academic scholarships for students who have done well in high school and continue to do well in college. So incentives still play a part for secondary and above education, but perhaps in a little different vein than the direct cash incentives of his case studies.  I think his statement here best sums up the chapter, \\"In general, cash incentives seem to work better at getting people to show up for a specific event-a doctor's appointment or an injection-than at changing long-term habits and behaviors\\".<br /><br />How Markets Crowd Out Morals<br /><br />Actually this was my favorite chapter. What can money buy? Friends, Nobel Prizes, Academy Awards... These are a few examples of things Sandel says we all agree money can't buy. Though any more I would say the way you use your money could buy you many \\"Friends\\". Perhaps not in the true sense of the word, but definitely in some ways in which we use the word. \\"Economists hate gift giving\\" I was interested about this topic and discussed it with my wife on which she would rather have. I remember how when I was a child getting money was lame, but now at times I would much rather have money than a gift, perhaps I have become corrupted toward the social free market system. I was very surprised by how market incentives tainted civic/national duty. It was a wonderful example of market corruption, but my favorite part is his discussion of the crowding out of non market norms, and the commercialization effect. The rubs comes to intrinsic motivations and external ones, and \\"when people are engaged n an activity they consider intrinsically worthwhile, offering them money may weaken their motivation\\" which goes against the fundamental economic theory. At one of the most interesting parts of the book he describes Arrow's two tenants of the market, both of which are very interesting but the second is the one most intriguing. \\"Ethical behavior is a commodity that needs to be economized\\" or that \\"Markets, which rely on self-interest, spare us from using up the limited supply of virtue\\". Sandel and I both agree that this is a complete falsehood. He goes into some wonderful discussions on love and altruism, but I see that this economizing of virtues distorts the very meaning of the words and \\"corrupts\\" them as Sandel might say.<br /><br />Markets in Life and Death<br /><br />This chapter discusses some of the moral issues within insurance and the difference between it and death bets. Much of this I didn't know about. I think it shows my own fallenness that as I am reading some of this and thinking how horrible it is, I am also thinking, \\"I wonder if I can make any money here\\".<br /><br />Naming Rights<br /><br />His last chapter mainly discussed advertising and where it should and should not be. While the arguments towards it were slightly on the fairness angle, most dealt with the corruption factor and degradation of the items, places, cars, or school that implemented the advertising. Woven within this chapter is also a theme he has sparsely throughout the book and that is the growing distance between the wealthy and the poor. One thing I think he notices and talks about which I very much admired is that the issue is not so much that certain people now have more money than others do, whether or not that is truer now of today than the past, but rather that the wealthy seems to live in a different world than the rest of us do. At the end of the chapter he calls it skyboxification. Where \\"the more things that money can buy, the fewer the occasions when people from different walks of life encounter one another\\". Perhaps we wouldn't really care to much if others had more money than us if they didn't or couldn't use it to separate themselves into their own world. Shouldn't we all be in this thing called life together anyway? I think this is a great aspect and personally I think it is one where the church can speak volumes. In the church community it shouldn't matter if you have money or not, we are all on the same level before God and all need each other in this \\"common life\\".
B004OZOTSQ|5.0|Overall: Basically, in a nutshell, this app allows you to connect and download from/upload to your tablet without a cord. It also allows you to delete files, change file names, simply everything you can do when you hook it up to the computer. Really useful if you, well, don't want to connect your tablet to the PC physically. Honestly though, this can be handy if say, you want to keep using your tablet while uploading a large file to it from the computer. The way the app works is that you open the app and then access the tablet file system through your web browser on your computer using an address that the app provides. Pretty simple, even for the technologically challenged. And the whole thing happens through your WiFi network. I was a little concerned about having difficulties at work; However, I was successful during my first attempt with zero troubleshooting. Very nice!<br /><br />Pros: First of all, I have used a couple others of these so called `USB Thumb Drive' apps and this one is definitely the best. It works well every time, over pretty much any network. This app is extremely useful because my tablet's physical connection basically prevents any use of the tablet when hooked up via USB. The app keeps running in the background, so all you have to do is set up an operation (upload/download) and then you keep using the tablet as normal. A password can be set up to access the webapp, which is a very nice feature.<br /><br />Cons: Webapp interface is slightly slow and will keep you waiting almost as if you were using dial up (not good if you are impatient). Visually speaking, the web interface is not as pretty as some of this app's competitors. Uploading is performed using a very antiquated system in my opinion. It seems that in this world of HTML 5 and Java powered upload tools, one could be added.<br /><br />Most Useful Feature: Downloading from the device is dead simple. In fact, it is the only action that requires a single mouse click from your browser. Simply locate the file you want, click it and it begins downloading to your computer immediately.

B007N0REX6|5.0|'Apocalyptic Love' is credited as Slash's second solo album, but in truth, it's more like like the debut album of his new band. After a number of talented-but-unstable frontmen, Slash finally seems to have found his perfect musical partner in Myles Kennedy. The tight and propulsive rhythm section of Todd Kerns and Brent Fitz is a force to be reckoned with as well, rounding out \\"The Conspirators\\". The results of this collaboration is a thoroughly enjoyable hard rock record that marks some of the best work of all the players involved.<br /><br />It may have taken decades in the business and several different bands along the way before finding each other, but Myles and Slash both compliment and push each other in mutually beneficial and interesting ways. Though his work in Alter Bridge is stellar, that band plays more to Kennedy's heavier, more metal sensibilities; with Slash, Kennedy gets to show off his looser, bluesier, rock 'n' roll side. Myles' voice is, quite simply, a thing of beauty, and the man is capable of singing just about anything. His assortment of high-pitched wails, soulful crooning, and lower-register vocals are the perfect compliment to Slash's playing, which, as usual, is phenomenal and instantly recognizable.<br /><br />The album is a straight-up blast of hard rock, the kind Slash lives to play: melodic, riff-heavy, and with an abundance of his trademark solos. The joy behind the album is pretty apparent in every track, as well it should be; for the first time in years, perhaps ever, Slash is playing in a band that doesn't seem on the verge of imploding at any given moment. Amazingly, none of the album's 13 tracks (15 in the Deluxe Edition) feel like filler, for each of them carry their own groove and energy. Particular standouts include the title track, Standing in the Sun, You're a Lie, No More Heroes, Anastasia, and Far and Away, though in all honesty, these selections are merely personal preference. As others have mentioned, virtually any one of the tracks could be a viable candidate for a single. The album really is that good.<br /><br />Most of the few negative reviews of this album seem to criticize it for what that particular reviewer wished it was, rather than what it actually is. Those who complain that Slash isn't \\"pushing his boundaries\\" or \\"experimenting\\" with his music are not only completely missing the point, they probably aren't very big fans of Slash in the first place. With 'Apocalyptic Love,' Slash has delivered precisely the type of album he loves to make: exciting, high volume, straight-up hard rock. With Myles Kennedy and The Conspirators, he has found the perfect band to realize his vision, and the results are not to be missed. Every member of the band has reason to be supremely proud of this record, and any fans of Slash, Myles Kennedy, or rock 'n' roll in general simply must check it out.
